            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of their abundance, Na and Ca can be attractive in ion batteries for large-scale grid storage.  However, many of the anode materials being pursued have limitations including vol. expansion, lack of passivating films, and slow kinetics.  Here, the authors study the adsorption of Na and Ca on graphene with di-vacancy and Stone-Wales defects in graphene.  Although adsorption of Na and Ca is not possible on pristine graphene, enhanced adsorption is obsd. on defective graphene because of increased charge transfer between the adatoms and defects.  The capacity of graphene increases with the d. of the defects.  For the max. possible di-vacancy defect densities, capacities of 1450 and 2900 mA-h/g for Na- and Ca-ion batteries, resp., can be achieved.  For Stone-Wales defects, max. capacities of 1071 and 2142 mA-h/g for Na and Ca, resp. were found.  The results provide guidelines to create better high-capacity anode materials for Na- and Ca-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Density Sodium and Lithium Ion Battery Anodes from Banana Peels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Ding, Jia; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Kalisvaart, W. Peter; Hazelton, Michael; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7115-7129</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. ∼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material ∼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. ∼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat ∼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with ∼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material ∼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode ∼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sushko, M.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Insertion in Hollow Carbon Nanowires for Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Lifen; Sushko, Maria L.; Wang, Wei; Schwenzer, Birgit; Xiao, Jie; Nie, Zimin; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Yang, Zhengguo; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Nanosheet Frameworks Derived from Peat Moss as High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Cui, Kai; Xu, Zhanwei; Zahiri, Beniamin; Tan, Xuehai; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Olsen, Brian C.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11004-11015</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/nn400601y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Culver, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Designed as a high-capacity alloy host for Na-ion chem., a forest of Sn nanorods with a unique core-shell structure was synthesized on viral scaffolds, which were genetically engineered to ensure a nearly vertical alignment upon self-assembly onto a metal substrate.  The interdigital spaces thus formed between individual rods effectively accommodated the vol. expansion and contraction of the alloy upon sodiation/desodiation, while addnl. carbon-coating engineered over these nanorods further suppressed Sn aggregation during extended electrochem. cycling.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical properties and morphological evolution of pitaya-like Sb@C microspheres as high-performance anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Lin; Lu, Haiyan; Xiao, Lifen; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pitaya-like Sb@C microspheres are prepd. successfully by facile aerosol spray drying synthesis. The Sb@C microsphere electrodes exhibit high Na storage capacity of 655 mA h g-1 at C/15 with excellent cyclability (93% of capacity retention over 100 cycles), as well as good rate capability.  Also, the morphol. evolution of the Sb@C microspheres is studied to account for its excellent electrochem. performance, caused by maintenance of the pitaya-like configuration during cycling.  This structural stability guarantees tight contact of Sb with C buffer, as well as uniform distribution of Sb to balance the localized mech. stress, ensuring excellent electrochem. performance.  The structural design and synthetic method reported in this work may provide an effective way to stabilize electrochem. performance of Na-storable alloy materials and therefore provide a new prospect for creation of cycle-stable alloy anodes for high capacity Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.03.043"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Interconnected carbon nanofibers networks (ICNNs) prepd. through the carbonization of polypyrrole (PPy) precursor are used as conductive pathways and buffer to improve the Na storage performance of antimony (Sb) as anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Flexible and Binder-Free Electrodes of Sb/rGO and Na3V2(PO4)3/rGO Nanocomposites for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Wei; Liu, Yangtao; Chen, Chaoji; Li, Zhen; Huang, Yunhui; Hu, Xianluo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">31</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3822-3829</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible power sources showed great promise in next-generation bendable, implantable, and wearable electronic systems.  Here, flexible and binder-free electrodes of Na3V2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide (NVP/rGO) and Sb/rGO nanocomposites for Na-ion batteries are reported.  The Sb/rGO and NVP/rGO paper electrodes with high flexibility and tailorability can be easily fabricated.  Sb and NVP nanoparticles are embedded homogeneously in the interconnected framework of rGO nanosheets, which provides structurally stable hosts for Na-ion intercalation and deintercalation.  The NVP/rGO paper-like cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 113 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and high capacity retention of ≈96.6% after 120 cycles.  The Sb/rGO paper-like anode gives a highly reversible capacity of 612 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1, an excellent rate capacity up to 30 C, and a good cycle performance.  Also, the Na-ion full cell of NVP/rGO//Sb/rGO was fabricated, delivering a highly reversible capacity of ≈400 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 charge/discharge cycles.  This work may provide promising electrode candidates for developing next-generation energy-storage devices with high capacity and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/nn4025674"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun Sb/C Fibers for a Stable and Fast Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Nano</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">6378</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">6386</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrospun Sb/C Fibers for a Stable and Fast Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yujie; Han, Xiaogang; Xu, Yunhua; Liu, Yihang; Zheng, Shiyou; Xu, Kang; Hu, Liangbing; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6378-6386</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the development of anode materials for SIBs to date was mainly limited to carbonaceous materials with minimal research devoted to high capacity alloy-based materials.  An Sb/C electrode with ∼30 nm Sb nanoparticles (NPs) uniformly encapsulated in interconnecting 1-dimensional (1D) 400 nm C fibers (denoted as SbNP@C) was fabricated using a simple and scalable electrospinning method.  This binder-free, current collector-free SbNP@C electrode demonstrated high capacity and stable long-term cycling performance at various current densities.  The SbNP@C electrode showed an initial total capacity of 422 mA-h/gelectrode and retained 350 mA-h/gelectrode after 300 deep charge-discharge cycles under 100 mA/gSb.  Also, because of the efficient 1-dimensional Na-ion transport pathway and the highly conductive network of SbNP@C, the electrode preserved high overall capacities even when cycled at high currents, extending its usability to high power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1039/c4cp01240b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, I.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cu2Sb thin films prepd. by magnetron sputtering are evaluated as an anode material for Na-ion batteries.  The starting material is composed of nanocrystallites with the desired tetragonal P4/nmm structure.  The study of the reaction mechanism reveals the formation of an amorphous/nanocryst. phase of compn. close to Na3Sb as the final reaction product.  The solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) material is mostly composed of carbonates (Na2CO3, NaCO3R) and possibly ethers.  The Cu2Sb anode possesses moderate capacity retention with a reversible storage capacity (250 mAh/g or 2100 mAh/cm3) close to the theor. value (323 mAh/g), an av. reaction potential of around 0.55 V vs.  Na/Na+, and a high rate performance (10 C-rate).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1039/c2cc17129e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kovarik, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nie, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity, Reversible Alloying Reactions in SnSb/C Nanocomposites for Na-ion Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. To improve the contact resistance that arose from the Schottky barrier between the metal electrodes and the single-walled carbon nanotubes (SWCNTs), electroless deposition of gold that has a higher work function than Al was adopted because the SWCNTs has p-type semiconductor properties. The SWCNT networks formed on the Au plated chip by a dip-coating method showed improved contact resistance and resistance variation between the Au electrode and SWCNTs.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/ja310347x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Darwiche, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Marino, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sougrati, M. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fraisse, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stievano, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Monconduit, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Am. Chem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">134</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">20805</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">20811</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja310347x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D134%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D20805-20811%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DA.%2BDarwiche%26author%3DC.%2BMarino%26author%3DM.%2BT.%2BSougrati%26author%3DB.%2BFraisse%26author%3DL.%2BStievano%26author%3DL.%2BMonconduit%26title%3DBetter%2BCycling%2BPerformances%2Bof%2BBulk%2BSb%2Bin%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%2BCompared%2Bto%2BLi-Ion%2BSystems%253A%2BAn%2BUnexpected%2BElectrochemical%2BMechanism%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fja310347x&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b10182&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja310347x&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Darwiche, Ali; Marino, Cyril; Sougrati, Moulay T.; Fraisse, Bernard; Stievano, Lorenzo; Monconduit, Laure</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">51</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20805-20811</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).  In addn., fluoroethylene carbonate added to the electrolyte combined with an appropriate electrode formulation based on CM-cellulose, carbon black, and vapor ground carbon fibers seems to be determinant in the excellent performances of this material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1007/s10086-002-0462-1"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamauchi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kurimoto, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Spectroscopic Study on Pyrolyzed Wood and Bark of Japanese Cedar: Ttemperature Dependence of Raman Parameters</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Wood Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">49</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">235</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s10086-002-0462-1</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2003%26pages%3D235-240%26journal%3DJ.%2BWood%2BSci.%26author%3DS.%2BYamauchi%26author%3DY.%2BKurimoto%26title%3DRaman%2BSpectroscopic%2BStudy%2Bon%2BPyrolyzed%2BWood%2Band%2BBark%2Bof%2BJapanese%2BCedar%253A%2BTtemperature%2BDependence%2Bof%2BRaman%2BParameters%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs10086-002-0462-1&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b10182&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs10086-002-0462-1&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman spectroscopic study on pyrolyzed wood and bark of Japanese cedar: temperature dependence of Raman parameters</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamauchi, Shigeru; Kurimoto, Yasuji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Wood Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2003</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">235-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JWSCFG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1435-0211</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer-Verlag Tokyo</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Raman spectra were measured for Japanese cedar wood and bark pyrolyzed in a nitrogen atm. at various temps. (200 - 1100 °C).  Two characteristic bands, near 1340 and 1590 cm-1, denoted as the D-band and G-band due to graphitic carbon, resp., appeared on all the spectra: and the spectral features changed markedly with increasing heat-treatment temp. (HTT).  The Raman parameters (band position, band width, DIG ratio) of the bands were deconvolved using of a curve-fitting method.  There was no significant difference in the values of the parameters between the wood and bark.  The D-band position and the G-band width showed a marked HTT dependence in the region of 400 - 800 °C.  We described the correlations of the Raman parameters with HTT and studied the availability of Raman spectroscopy as a means for evaluating HTT.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886455d4954b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201402511"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ha, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This work presents an up-to-date information on Na-based battery materials.  On the one hand, it explores the feasibility of two novel energy storage systems: Na-aq. batteries and Na-O2 technol.  On the other hand, it summarizes new advances on non-aq. Na-ion systems.  Although all of them can be placed under the umbrella of Na-based systems, aq. and oxygen-based batteries are arising technologies with increasing significance in energy storage research, while non-aq. sodium-ion technol. has become one of the most important research lines in this field.  These systems meet different requirements of energy storage: Na-aq. batteries will have a detg. role as a low cost and safer technol.; Na-O2 systems can be the key technol. to overcome the need for high energy d. storage devices; and non-aq. Na-ion batteries have application in the field of stationary energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, S.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1021/nl404165c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kravchyk, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Walter, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kovalenko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of their abundance, Na and Ca can be attractive in ion batteries for large-scale grid storage.  However, many of the anode materials being pursued have limitations including vol. expansion, lack of passivating films, and slow kinetics.  Here, the authors study the adsorption of Na and Ca on graphene with di-vacancy and Stone-Wales defects in graphene.  Although adsorption of Na and Ca is not possible on pristine graphene, enhanced adsorption is obsd. on defective graphene because of increased charge transfer between the adatoms and defects.  The capacity of graphene increases with the d. of the defects.  For the max. possible di-vacancy defect densities, capacities of 1450 and 2900 mA-h/g for Na- and Ca-ion batteries, resp., can be achieved.  For Stone-Wales defects, max. capacities of 1071 and 2142 mA-h/g for Na and Ca, resp. were found.  The results provide guidelines to create better high-capacity anode materials for Na- and Ca-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. ∼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material ∼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. ∼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat ∼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with ∼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material ∼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode ∼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sushko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/nn400601y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Culver, J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible power sources showed great promise in next-generation bendable, implantable, and wearable electronic systems.  Here, flexible and binder-free electrodes of Na3V2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide (NVP/rGO) and Sb/rGO nanocomposites for Na-ion batteries are reported.  The Sb/rGO and NVP/rGO paper electrodes with high flexibility and tailorability can be easily fabricated.  Sb and NVP nanoparticles are embedded homogeneously in the interconnected framework of rGO nanosheets, which provides structurally stable hosts for Na-ion intercalation and deintercalation.  The NVP/rGO paper-like cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 113 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and high capacity retention of ≈96.6% after 120 cycles.  The Sb/rGO paper-like anode gives a highly reversible capacity of 612 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1, an excellent rate capacity up to 30 C, and a good cycle performance.  Also, the Na-ion full cell of NVP/rGO//Sb/rGO was fabricated, delivering a highly reversible capacity of ≈400 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 charge/discharge cycles.  This work may provide promising electrode candidates for developing next-generation energy-storage devices with high capacity and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1021/nn4025674"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun Sb/C Fibers for a Stable and Fast Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Nano</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">6378</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">6386</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the development of anode materials for SIBs to date was mainly limited to carbonaceous materials with minimal research devoted to high capacity alloy-based materials.  An Sb/C electrode with ∼30 nm Sb nanoparticles (NPs) uniformly encapsulated in interconnecting 1-dimensional (1D) 400 nm C fibers (denoted as SbNP@C) was fabricated using a simple and scalable electrospinning method.  This binder-free, current collector-free SbNP@C electrode demonstrated high capacity and stable long-term cycling performance at various current densities.  The SbNP@C electrode showed an initial total capacity of 422 mA-h/gelectrode and retained 350 mA-h/gelectrode after 300 deep charge-discharge cycles under 100 mA/gSb. CMOS process compatible Al patterned chips were used as substrates for easy incorporation with existing CMOS circuits.  To improve the contact resistance that arose from the Schottky barrier between the metal electrodes and the single-walled carbon nanotubes (SWCNTs), electroless deposition of gold that has a higher work function than Al was adopted because the SWCNTs has p-type semiconductor properties. The SWCNT networks formed on the Au plated chip by a dip-coating method showed improved contact resistance and resistance variation between the Au electrode and SWCNTs.                         rightTab-cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb-C nanofibers with long cycle life as an anode material for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Lin; Hu, Xiaohong; Qian, Jiangfeng; Pei, Feng; Wu, Fayuan; Mao, Rongjun; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">323-328</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb-C nanofibers are synthesized successfully through a single-nozzle electrospinning technique and subsequent calcination.  The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal the uniform nanofiber structure with the Sb nanoparticles embedded homogeneously in the carbon nanofibers.  Electrochem. expts. show that the Sb-C nanofiber electrode can deliver large reversible capacity (631 mA h g-1) at C/15, greatly improved rate capability (337 mA h g-1 at 5 C) and excellent cycling stability (90% capacity retention after 400 cycles).  The superior electrochem. performances of the Sb-C nanofibers are due to the unique nanofiber structure and uniform distribution of Sb nanoparticles in carbon matrix, which provides a conductive and buffering matrix for effective release of mech. stress caused by Na ion insertion/extn. and prevent the aggregation of the Sb nanoparticles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06476C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H. S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y. C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y. R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, W. X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X. M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X. B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sb Porous Hollow Microspheres as Advanced Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Mater. Chem. A</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">3</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2971</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2977</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4TA06476C</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb porous hollow microspheres as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou, Hongshuai; Jing, Mingjun; Yang, Yingchang; Zhang, Yan; Zhu, Yirong; Song, Weixin; Yang, Xuming; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2971-2977</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb porous hollow microspheres (PHMSs) were prepd. by a replacement reaction employing Zn microspheres (MSs) as templates.  The obtained Sb PHMSs were first applied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and showed a high reversible capacity of 617 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles, exhibiting a high capacity retention of 97.2%.  Even at a high c.d. of 3200 mA g-1, the reversible capacity can also reach 312.9 mA h g-1.  The superior electrochem. performance of Sb PHMSs can be attributed to the unique structural characteristic of Sb with porous and hollow structure, which can accommodate the vol. change and facilitate the Na+ diffusion during the sodiation and desodiation process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201300872"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xue, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Nanoparticles Embedded in Uniform Mesoporous Carbon Spheres for Superior High-Rate Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Funct. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">24</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">319</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">326</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201300872</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fe3O4 Nanoparticles Embedded in Uniform Mesoporous Carbon Spheres for Superior High-Rate Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Yu; Song, Bohang; Li, Meng; Lu, Li; Xue, Junmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">319-326</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Robust composite structures consisting of Fe3O4 nanoparticles (IONP) (∼5 nm) embedded in mesoporous carbon spheres with an av. size of about 70 nm (IONP@mC) are synthesized by a facile two-step method: uniform Fe3O4 nanoparticles are first synthesized followed by a post-synthetic low-temp. hydrothermal step to encapsulate them in mesoporous carbon spheres.  Instead of graphene which has been extensively reported for use in high-rate battery applications as a carbonaceous material combined with metal oxides mesoporous carbon is chosen to enhance the overall performances.  The interconnecting pores facilitate the penetration of electrolyte leading to direct contact between electrochem. active Fe3O4 and lithium ion-carrying electrolyte greatly facilitating lithium ion transportation.  The interconnecting carbon framework provides continuous 3D electron transportation routes.  The anodes fabricated from IONP@mC are cycled under high current densities ranging from 500 to 10 000 mA g-1.  A high reversible capacity of 271 mAh g-1 is reached at 10, 000 mAh g-1 demonstrating its superior high rate performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA06856D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Encapsulation of Selenium in Porous Hollow Carbon Spheres for Advanced Lithium-Selenium Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">RSC Adv.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">4</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">39312</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">39315</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4RA06856D</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Encapsulation of selenium in porous hollow carbon spheres for advanced lithium-selenium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lai, Yanqing; Yang, Fuhua; Zhang, Zhian; Jiang, Shaofeng; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">74</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">39312-39315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous hollow carbon spheres (PHCSs) with high surface area and large pore vol. were synthesized using a facile template method.  After selenium (Se) encapsulation, Se/PHCS composite was formed.  The obtained Se/PHCS composite was demonstrated as a suitable cathode material for lithium-selenium batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/cm7031288"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Demir Cakan, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y. S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Antonietti, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maier, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Titirici, M. M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile One-Pot Synthesis of Mesoporous SnO<sub>2</sub> Microspheres via Nanoparticles Assembly and Lithium Storage Properties</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">20</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1227</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1229</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/cm7031288</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1021/ja310347x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Darwiche, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Marino, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sougrati, M. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fraisse, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stievano, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Monconduit, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Am. Chem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">134</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">20805</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">20811</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja310347x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Darwiche, Ali; Marino, Cyril; Sougrati, Moulay T.; Fraisse, Bernard; Stievano, Lorenzo; Monconduit, Laure</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">51</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20805-20811</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).  In addn., fluoroethylene carbonate added to the electrolyte combined with an appropriate electrode formulation based on CM-cellulose, carbon black, and vapor ground carbon fibers seems to be determinant in the excellent performances of this material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1007/s10086-002-0462-1"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamauchi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kurimoto, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Spectroscopic Study on Pyrolyzed Wood and Bark of Japanese Cedar: Ttemperature Dependence of Raman Parameters</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Wood Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">49</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">235</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s10086-002-0462-1</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman spectroscopic study on pyrolyzed wood and bark of Japanese cedar: temperature dependence of Raman parameters</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamauchi, Shigeru; Kurimoto, Yasuji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Wood Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2003</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">235-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JWSCFG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1435-0211</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer-Verlag Tokyo</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Raman spectra were measured for Japanese cedar wood and bark pyrolyzed in a nitrogen atm. at various temps. (200 - 1100 °C).  Two characteristic bands, near 1340 and 1590 cm-1, denoted as the D-band and G-band due to graphitic carbon, resp., appeared on all the spectra: and the spectral features changed markedly with increasing heat-treatment temp. (HTT).  The Raman parameters (band position, band width, DIG ratio) of the bands were deconvolved using of a curve-fitting method.  There was no significant difference in the values of the parameters between the wood and bark.  The D-band position and the G-band width showed a marked HTT dependence in the region of 400 - 800 °C.  We described the correlations of the Raman parameters with HTT and studied the availability of Raman spectroscopy as a means for evaluating HTT.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i8">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b10182" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b10182</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional details and figures as mentioned in the text (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10182/suppl_file/am5b10182_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10182/suppl_file/am5b10182_si_001.pdf">am5b10182_si_001.pdf (3.73 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="931886455d4954b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="931886455d4954b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" type="text/css" href="/pb-assets/smartgroup_ads/Modal-LeadGen-1691422642793.css">
            
        </div>

<script nonce="931886455d4954b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);

        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>

        <script nonce="931886455d4954b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
